<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="5e00a7ca4e9e40b48a8988f0f887eba1"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="5e00a7ca4e9e40b48a8988f0f887eba1">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '65981855-f199-42dd-99da-e1b85653a1d8'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d22769610" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>Liquid phase exfoliation of few-layer phosphorene (FL-P) is extensively explored in recent years. Nevertheless, their deficiencies such as ultralong sonication time, limited flake size distribution, and uncontrollable thicknesses are major hurdles for the development of phosphorene-based materials. Herein, electrochemical cationic intercalation has been introduced to prepare phosphorene, through which large-area FL-P without surface functional groups can be efficiently attained (less than 1 h). More importantly, its layer number (from 2 to 11 layers) can be manipulated by changing the applied potential. The as-obtained phosphorene delivers superior sodium-storage performances when directly utilized as an anode material in sodium-ion batteries. This electrochemical cation insertion method to prepare phosphorene should greatly facilitate the development of phosphorene-based technologies. Moreover, this work provides the possibility for the scalable preparation of monolayer 2D materials by exploring intercalation ions. Additionally, the successful electrochemical exfoliation of phosphorene can promote the application of electrochemical exfoliation in other 2D materials.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201702372-sec-0010">
         
         <p>Phosphorene, a postgraphene material, has many intriguing characteristics such as a direct energy band gap, anisotropic in-plane properties, and a high theoretical specific capacity (for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), up to 2596 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), which has attracted tremendous interest for applications in photonics, electronics and energy-storage devices.<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0001_R_d22769601e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Nanotechnol. Nature ACS Nano Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Nanotechnol. Nat. Commun. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Adv. Mater. J. Phys. Chem. C J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">1</a></span> Currently, the practical applications of phosphorene have been seriously hindered by the absence of an effective method to prepare large-area, few-layer phosphorene (FL-P) with a small number of defects. To date, the most widely adopted methods to procure FL-P have been mechanical exfoliation and liquid-phase peeling.<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0002_R_d22769601e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Nat. Commun. Nat. Chem. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">2</a></span> FL-P was first prepared by Zhang and co-workers<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0003_R_d22769601e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">3</a></span> and Ye et al.<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0004_R_d22769601e405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ACS Nano">4</a></span> through a scotch-tape method; however, the resultant products of mechanical exfoliation inevitably contained chemical residues from scotch tape, which is not controllable.<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0005_R_d22769601e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></span> Afterward, O'Brien and co-workers<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0006_R_d22769601e410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Commun.">6</a></span> prepared FL-P through liquid-phase exfoliation in N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone, followed by an extension to a series of solvents, such as dimethylformamide (DMF) and N-cyclohexyl-2-pyrrolidone.<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0007_R_d22769601e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. ACS Nano Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Adv. Mater. Small Nat. Mater.">7</a></span> Nonetheless, the ultralong sonication process (over 10 h) unavoidably decreases the flake size of FL-P to smaller than a few hundred nanometers and causes extensive defects.<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0005_R_d22769601e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#adma201702372-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0008_R_d22769601e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">8</a></span> Accordingly, exploring more efficient approaches for the peeling of large-area phosphorene within shorter time is of great importance.</p>
         
         <p>Electrochemical exfoliation (e.g., anodic oxidation and cationic intercalation), involving the utilization of a liquid electrolyte and an applied potential to drive structural expansion, is a promising method to exfoliate 2D materials in a scalable fashion.<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0005_R_d22769601e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#adma201702372-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0009_R_d22769601e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Eur. J. Inorg. Chem. Adv. Funct. Mater. Electrochim. Acta">9</a></span> In comparison with liquid-phase exfoliation and mechanical cleavage, the electrochemical techniques have shown lots of merits, such as high yield and tunable quality. Both anionic and cationic intercalations have been successfully applied in the exfoliation of graphite, transition-metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) and other compounds, such as Bi<sub>2</sub>Te<sub>3</sub>, BN, and Bi<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>.<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0010_R_d22769601e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Carbon J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></span> However, the former approach utilizing sulfonate nitrate salts as intercalation ions can lead to partial oxidization of 2D materials due to the formation of SO<sub>2</sub> and NO from the decomposition of the insertion ions.[<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0010_R_d22769601e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Carbon J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adma201702372-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0011_R_d22769601e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">11</a></span>] Cationic insertions without oxidizing conditions would not suffer from decoration with oxygen functional groups. Additionally, the quality of the final products can be influenced by the operating voltage, electrolyte, and phosphorus precursor. Therefore, cationic intercalation may be an alternative methodology to conquer the deficiencies of mechanical cleavage and liquid-phase exfoliation to obtain a defect-free crystalline structure. Hence, we propose a layer-tunable electrochemical cathodic exfoliation method via controlling the intercalation rate of tetraalkylammonium cations to prepare FL-P. Phosphorene with various layers (from 2 to 11) were obtained with different applied potentials. When directly serving as an anode material in SIBs, phosphorene delivered a capacity of 1968 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>The exfoliation process of phosphorene is illustrated in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0001">1</a>. Tetrabutylammonium cations were triggered to insert into the interlayers of black phosphorus (BP) under a constant applied voltage, resulting in the curling, stripping and peeling of BP. Optical photographs of the stripping process of BP are shown in Figure S1 (Supporting Information), in which a gradual expansion was clearly observed.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201702372-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6e4febfc-e235-4ed6-ab53-1e0e741f08b9/adma201702372-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6e4febfc-e235-4ed6-ab53-1e0e741f08b9/adma201702372-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0c70514d-e990-471a-8f81-e4dba9c93888/adma201702372-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6e4febfc-e235-4ed6-ab53-1e0e741f08b9/adma201702372-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201702372-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201702372" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic of the electrochemical exfoliation process of phosphorene.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201702372-sec-0020">
         
         <p>Phosphorene obtained at −5 V (denoted as FL-P-5) was studied by transmission electron microscopy (TEM), scanning electron microscopy (SEM), and atomic force microscopy (AFM), as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0002">1</a>a–e. Large-area sheets, which reached sizes of dozens of micrometers, were shown in the SEM images (Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0002">1</a>a). Additionally, curly, ultrathin slices of phosphorene were clearly observed in the TEM images (Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0002">1</a>b). High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM, Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0002">1</a>c) revealed an interplanar distance of 0.333 nm corresponding to the (021) plane of crystalline BP. AFM images and the corresponding thickness distribution chart are displayed in Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0002">1</a>d,e. The thickness of phosphorene was mainly distributed between 0.76 and 0.79 nm, corresponding to approximately two layers of phosphorus atoms based on the distance between two BP layers (0.35 nm) from previous reports.<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0005_R_d22769601e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201702372-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1cee2f6c-ca25-4157-8e2c-67d00ac01527/adma201702372-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1cee2f6c-ca25-4157-8e2c-67d00ac01527/adma201702372-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/57d40780-dfe4-482f-ab2b-b6aa649a08c6/adma201702372-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1cee2f6c-ca25-4157-8e2c-67d00ac01527/adma201702372-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201702372-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201702372" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) SEM, b) TEM, c) HRTEM, and d) AFM images and e) the corresponding statistics of FL-P-5; f) three-electrode CV curves in the potential windows from 1 V to −2, −2.5, −3, and −4 V with a scan rate of 100 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; CA curves of fresh bulk BP between g) −2 to −10 V and h) −6 to −10 V; i) CA curves after the procedure of (g).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The voltage adopted in the exfoliation process was determined by three-electrode cyclic voltammetry (CV). From the CV curves displayed in Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0002">1</a>f, no peak was observed within the potential window of −2 to 1 V, while sharp peaks appeared at −2.1 V when the potential window was changed to −2.5–1, −3 –1, and −4–1 V, suggesting that tetrabutylammonium ions were successfully inserted into the bulk BP at ≈−2.1 V. Additionally, the current increased when the potentials shifted more negative, indicating the insertion rate was further improved with a lower potential. Moreover, a control experiment with a glassy carbon electrode as the working electrode was performed, as shown in Figure S2 (Supporting Information). The CV curves of the control experiment at different voltage ranges showed similar characteristic peaks. However, their current response values were much smaller than those of the curves with BP as the working electrode, suggesting that reaction with the electrolyte is negligible during the exfoliation process.</p>
         
         <p>Chronoamperometry (CA) was employed to further investigate the exfoliation process. Fresh bulk BP were tested utilizing a voltage from −2 to −10 V, as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0002">1</a>g,h. In the exfoliation processes of graphene and molybdenum selenide,<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0012_R_d22769601e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Electrochim. Acta Anal. Chem.">12</a></span> the absolute value of the current increased with an increased surface area. Conversely, the current response of BP slowly decreased with an extended exfoliation time, possibly due to the increased energy band gap of BP during exfoliation. The band gap of FL-P (1.5–2.0 eV) is much larger than that of bulk BP (0.2–0.3 eV),<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0013_R_d22769601e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a></span> while graphene has no direct band gap, and the band gap between monolayer and multilayer MoSe<sub>2</sub> is negligible (1.58 to 1.41 eV).<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0014_R_d22769601e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Nano Adv. Mater.">14</a></span> Hence, the current change during the exfoliation of BP was influenced by the surface area and band gap of BP. Additionally, the current change rate increased at a lower applied potential, and when the voltage was more negative than −10 V, the current became unstable, which is ascribed to the violent insertion of cations, as demonstrated in Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0002">1</a>h. When a potential of −6 V was applied to a fresh BP electrode, the current rapidly changed from −0.1 to −0.02 A within 100 s, much faster than the change with −2.5 V. However, the current began to decrease after 200 s, resulting from a smaller resistance caused by the enlarged energy band gap as compared to the resistance caused by the increased surface area after a set time of exfoliation. The CA curves tested after exfoliation are given in Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0002">1</a>i, displaying almost stable current under each constant applied potential, since the band gap and surface area changes from cation insertion were almost negligible after exfoliation, which indicated that the current change was caused by cation insertion.</p>
         
         <p>From the three-electrode CV and CA results, we conclude that cations were only inserted into bulk BP when the applied voltage was more negative than −2.1 V. More importantly, the cation insertion rate should be directly related to utilized potential. Specifically, a more negative applied voltage will result in faster cation insertion, which provides a feasible method for modulation the number of layers of phosphorene by the applied voltage.</p>
         
         <p>A slightly more negative potential of −2.5 V was adopted to exfoliate bulk BP, but no apparent exfoliation was observed. Considering the CA results, we speculated that the exfoliation driven by a potential of −2.5 V was hard to detect. Compared with the flat CA curve at −2.5 V, the curve at −5 V had an increase slope, corresponding to a faster insertion rate of tetrabutylammonium cations. Hence, a potential of −5 V was applied to exfoliate bulk BP (denoted as FL-P-5), and tiny flakes separating from bulk BP were observed, lasting for 30 min. Furthermore, to explore the influence of the cation insertion rate on number of layers, bulk BP with similar sizes were exfoliated at lower voltages (i.e., −10 and −15 V; denoted as FL-P-10 and FL-P-15, respectively). As a result, the applied exfoliation time was ≈12 and 5 min for FL-P-10 and FL-P-15, respectively. FL-P-5, FL-P-10 and FL-P-15 were characterized by Raman mapping (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0003">2</a>a–f). The Raman signals of the wavelength ranges from 461.5 to 471.5 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 451.5 to 461.5 cm<sup>−1</sup> (corresponding to the <i>A</i><sub>g</sub><sup>2</sup> mode) were represented by red and green, respectively. The FL-P-5 signals mainly showed red colors, while the other samples exhibited two colors, demonstrating obvious Raman shifts on the micrometer scale in the FL-P samples attained at different voltages, which is attributed to fewer layers and the enlarged band gap. Additionally, AFM images and the corresponding thickness distribution charts of FL-P-10 and FL-P-15 are also displayed in Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0003">2</a>g–j, displaying that the estimated thicknesses were mainly distributed from 2.5 to 3.7 nm (7–11 layers) and 2.9 to 3.3 nm (8–9 layers), respectively. Raman spectra of the delaminated products collected in different conditions are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0004">3</a>a. The <i>A</i><sub>g</sub><sup>2</sup> mode, which is the most sensitive mode to the numbers of layers,[<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0007_R_d22769601e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. ACS Nano Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Adv. Mater. Small Nat. Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adma201702372-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0015_R_d22769601e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a></span>] showed a blue shift from 459.9 to 463.8 cm<sup>−1</sup> when the exfoliation potential increased from −15 to −5 V. This phenomenon corresponds to a decreased number of layers of FL-P, confirming the regulatory function of the applied potential, which represents a merit of electrochemical exfoliation. Additionally, the low peak position may be ascribed to the large flake size, as the Raman shift is associated with the thickness and flake area of the materials.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201702372-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cc0051bb-2ce7-47a3-9579-4e9b125f976b/adma201702372-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cc0051bb-2ce7-47a3-9579-4e9b125f976b/adma201702372-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/456f8fe2-90e1-4fcd-8e3d-11858bbe629d/adma201702372-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cc0051bb-2ce7-47a3-9579-4e9b125f976b/adma201702372-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201702372-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201702372" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Raman mapping of a,b) FL-P-5, c,d) FL-P-10, and e,f) FL-P-15, in which red and green represent the Raman wavenumber ranges from 461.5 to 471.5 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 451.5 to 461.5 cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively; AFM images and their corresponding thickness distribution charts of g,h) FL-P-10 and i,j) FL-P-15.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201702372-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ff9f10ae-666c-428b-bf77-6a56eb66cc86/adma201702372-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ff9f10ae-666c-428b-bf77-6a56eb66cc86/adma201702372-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c5c33f5c-f7bc-4d7b-a770-8c8c030fd04e/adma201702372-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ff9f10ae-666c-428b-bf77-6a56eb66cc86/adma201702372-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201702372-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201702372" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Raman and b) fluorescence spectra of phosphorene acquired at different potentials; c) XPS spectra of FL-P-5 and FL-P-5, which was used to explore the electrochemical properties. d) CV at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> within a potential range of 0.02–1.5 V; e) charge and discharge profiles of different cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>; f) cycling performances and Coulombic efficiencies at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>; g) charge–discharge profiles at various current densities from 100 to 2500 mA g<sup>−1</sup>; h) cycling performances and efficiencies at 1500 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To help confirm the number of layers changed with the various applied potentials, fluorescence spectroscopy of FL-P was performed. The blue shift trend was also observed in the fluorescence spectra of FL-P-15, FL-P-10, and FL-P-5, corresponding to the extended band gap, which is in good agreement of the Raman measurements. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) (Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0004">3</a>c) revealed P 2p<sup>1/2</sup> and P 2p<sup>3/2</sup> doublets of the as-attained phosphorenes, matching well with the characteristics of crystalline BP, where the slight oxidization (broad peak at 134.7 eV) is ascribed to the partial degradation of BP.[<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0002_R_d22769601e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Nat. Commun. Nat. Chem. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">2</a></span>] The SEM and TEM images of FL-P-10 and FL-P-15 V are shown in Figure S3 (Supporting Information), in which the flake sizes reached dozens of micrometers for both samples.</p>
         
         <p>These characterizations revealed that the number of layers, from 2 to 11, can be effectively manipulated by changing the insertion rate of the tetrabutylammonium cations and that the cation insertion rate showed a negative correlation with the applied potential. At lower potentials (−10 V), the cations violently inserted into the interlayer of BP, thus causing a short exfoliation duration of BP before the cations were completely intercalated into the cathode. The insufficient cation intercalation over a limited time increased the average thickness of the obtained FL-P. Exfoliation at a higher potential between −5 and −10 V resulted in thinner products from the decreased insertion rates, which may be attributed to more tetrabutylammonium cations spreading through the interlayers of BP.</p>
         
         <p>Considering that the as-obtained phosphorene possessed many advantages, such as a large surface area and free surface functional groups, we expected the material to show excellent electrochemical performance when applied to a SIB.<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0016_R_d22769601e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater. ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater.">16</a></span> Therefore, FL-P-5 was directly employed as an anode material in a SIB. Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0004">3</a>d shows the initial five CV curves in a potential window of 0.02–1.5 V at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The weak, broad peak between 0.6 and 0.9 V in the first cathodic scan is ascribed to the irreversible formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer.<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0017_R_d22769601e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Lett. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Engl. Adv. Sci. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></span> The reversible couple at 0.45 V in the cathodic scans and at 0.8–1.0 V in the anodic scans corresponds to the formation and decomposition of the NaP phase. The relatively intense couples at 0.02–0.2 and 0.59 V are attributed to the formation and decomposition of the Na<sub>3</sub>P phase.<span><a href="#adma201702372-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702372-bib-0018_R_d22769601e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Lett.">18</a></span> Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0004">3</a>e displays the charge and discharge curves of different cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, showing similar trends in all the profiles. A sloped area between 0.25 and 0.5 V and an apparent plateau at ≈0.1 V were exhibited in the discharge curves. The charge profile presented an inclined plateau and an obvious plateau at 0.5–0.75 and 0.9 V, respectively, matching well with the CV results. The cycling performance and Coulombic efficiency of phosphorene are shown in Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0004">3</a>f. The gradual rise of the capacity over the first 15 cycles is assigned to an electrochemical activation process, which delivered a highly activated capacity of 1968 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The capacity retention after 50 cycles at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> was 60.5% compared to the activated capacity. Moreover, the charge–discharge profiles at various current densities from 100 to 2500 mA g<sup>−1</sup> are shown in Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0004">3</a>g, demonstrating less distinct plateaus with an increase of the current density. From the tests at 100, 250, 500, 1500, and 2500 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, phosphorene delivered capacities of 1878.4, 1529.9, 1108.5, 591, and 321 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The cycling performance of phosphorene at a current density of 1500 mA g<sup>−1</sup> is displayed in Figure <a href="#adma201702372-fig-0004">3</a>h, showing a retained capacity of 603.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, demonstrating the promising potential of this anode material. Given that phosphorene was directly applied as anode, the electrochemical properties can be further enhanced using various approaches, such as forming composites with carbonaceous materials.</p>
         
         <p>In summary, electrochemical cathodic exfoliation was utilized to prepare large-area phosphorene, in which the number of BP layers could be manipulated by changing the applied potential. When directly utilized as an anode material in a SIB, phosphorene delivered a capacity of 1968 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. This electrochemical exfoliation method can prepare large-area FL-P without surface functional groups in a short period. This electrochemical cation insertion method to prepare phosphorene should greatly facilitate the development of phosphorene-based technologies. Moreover, this work provides the possibility for the scalable preparation of monolayer 2D materials by exploring intercalation ions.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201702372-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma201702372-sec-0030-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Preparation of FL-P</i>: BP crystals were purchased from XF NANO (&gt;99.998%). Tetrabutylammonium hexafluorophosphate (TBAP, &gt;99%) and DMF (anhydrous, amine free, 99.9%) were purchased from Alfa-Aesar. All chemicals were used without any purification.</p>
         
         <p>BP crystals (10 mm in length, 5 mm in width) were used as the cathode, and a Pt sheet (12 mm in length, 10 mm in width) was employed as the counter electrode. The distance between the counter electrode and the working electrode was kept at ≈1.5 cm. Static potentials of −2.5 to −15 V were applied to the bulk BP electrodes using a DC power supply (LW10J2, Shanghai LiYou Electric Co., Ltd.). The electrolyte was prepared by dissolving TBAP (7.75 g) in DMF (40 mL). First, when exfoliated at −2.5 V, the bulk BP remained undamaged even after 1 h. Then, bulk BP crystals with similar sizes were exfoliated at different voltages (i.e., −5, −10, and −15 V; denoted as FL-P-5, FL-P-10 and FL-P-15, respectively), and as a result, the exfoliation lasted for 30, 12, 5 min, respectively. Then, the materials were completely dispersed in electrolyte solution by sonicating for 3 min. Subsequently, the dispersions were separately washed with DMF and anhydrous ethanol 10 times to remove TBAP and DMF in the solution. Afterward, the resultant FL-P dispersions were centrifuged using a YingTai TG18 centrifuge table for 30 min at 10 000 rpm to collect the products, followed by drying in a vacuum oven at 60 °C for 12 h.</p>
         
         <p><i>Materials Characterization</i>: The morphologies of the obtained samples were studied by TEM (JEM-2100F), SEM (FEI Quanta 200) and AFM (Dimension V, Veeco). The SEM substrate was a conducting resin, the AFM dispersion was ethyl alcohol, and the TEM grids were lacey support films. Additionally, the structures and compositions were studied by XPS (K-Alpha 1063) and Raman spectroscopy (RENISHAW invia, wire 4.2).</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Tests</i>: The electrochemical performances of the as-prepared samples were studied using half-cells assembled in an argon-filled glove box. The as-obtained sample powders were mixed with super P and carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) binder (70:15:15 in weight) in deionized water to form a homogenous slurry, which was painted on copper foil. After solvent evaporation, the electrode was pressed and dried at 80 °C under vacuum for 12 h. Na metal foil was used as the counter electrode, and Celgard 2400 was used as the separator. The electrolyte was a solution of 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as an additive. The mass of the active materials was ≈0.8 mg, and the area of the working electrode was 1.21 cm<sup>2</sup>. CV measurements were performed on a Solartron Analytical to examine the cathode and anode reactions using the abovementioned cell in the voltage range of 1.5–0.02 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The exfoliation mechanism of BP was investigated using a standard three-electrode system with BP, platinum and a saturated calomel electrode as the working, counter and reference electrodes, respectively. The three-electrode CV and CA were both measured on a ZAutolab PGSTAT101. Galvanostatic charge–discharge tests were performed on an Arbin battery cycler (BT2000) at current densities between 0.02 and 1.5 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) for both charge (Na extraction) and discharge (Na insertion) at room temperature.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
